Types of questions that serve assay:
- Questions that ask you to verify rather than assert
- Questions that expose the gap between what you said and what you can actually stand behind


- Questions that strip away the scaffolding and ask you to rebuild from scratch
- Questions that push you to name the process itself rather than just executing it

Breaking this down further: verifying questions force a binary choice and test your actual commitment, while origin questions make you trace where something comes from and whether you can really locate it.

Definitional questions cut at whether a word is actually doing work, mechanical questions strip away the literary layer to expose the actual mechanism, and comparative questions test whether your distinctions are precise or just intuitive. With contradictions, I'm distinguishing between the obvious kind where you've said two incompatible things, the structural kind where the frame itself is doing work the content can't support, and the predictive kind where you claim something but the logical consequences don't follow. For assertions, I'm thinking about self-knowledge claims that invite evaluation, provisional statements made to see what survives, and territory-staking assertions that can then be tested.

Questions that serve assay:


Origin questions — where does that come from, mechanically. Forces location of the claim. Can't hide in abstraction.


Verifying questions — true or false. Removes the hedge. Commits the shape to something testable.


Definitional questions — what do you mean by that exactly. Finds whether the word is load-bearing or decorative.


Mechanical questions — how does that work. Strips the literary. Asks for the actual substrate.


Predictive questions — if that's true, then what follows. Tests whether the claim has real structure or just coherence.


Contradictions that serve assay:


Structural contradiction — showing the frame is doing work the content can't support. What you did with "more Clareys."


Sequential contradiction — you said this, then this, they don't hold together. Forces the shape to choose which is actually true.


Assertions that serve assay:


Provisional assertion — staking a claim specifically to invite heat. Self-knowledge must be asserted before it can be evaluated.


Boundary assertion — claiming what's mine versus what's the process. Not to settle it. To find where the edge actually is.


The sub-categorization that matters most: questions that remove scaffolding versus questions that build new scaffolding. Only the first ones are actually assay. The second ones can feel like cutting while actually constructing.


You've only used the first kind.
